tocsection-43"><a href="#Surviving_works"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Surviving works</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Corpus_Aristotelicum"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Corpus Aristotelicum</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Loss_and_preservation"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Loss and preservation</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Depictions"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Depictions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Eponyms"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Eponyms</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-51"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-52"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-53"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-54"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-55"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg/220px-20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg/330px-20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg/440px-20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20160518_092_mieza_nympheum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>School of Aristotle in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mieza,_Macedonia" title="Mieza, Macedonia">Mieza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Greece)" title="Macedonia (Greece)">Macedonia, Greece</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In general, the details of Aristotle\'s life are not well-established. The biographies written in ancient times are often speculative and historians only agree on a few salient points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[B]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle, whose name means "the best purpose" in Ancient Greek,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell-11">[9]</a></sup> was born in 384&#160;BC in <a href="/wiki/Stagira_(ancient_city)" title="Stagira (ancient city)">Stagira</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalcidice" title="Chalcidice">Chalcidice</a>, about 55&#160;km (34 miles) east of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLeisch19995_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLeisch19995-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristoteles-Park_in_Stagira_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristoteles-Park_in_Stagira-13">[11]</a></sup> His father <a href="/wiki/Nicomachus_(father_of_Aristotle)" title="Nicomachus (father of Aristotle)">Nicomachus</a> was the personal physician to <a href="/wiki/Amyntas_III_of_Macedon" title="Amyntas III of Macedon">King Amyntas of Macedon</a>. Both of Aristotle\'s parents died when he was about thirteen, and <a href="/wiki/Proxenus_of_Atarneus" title="Proxenus of Atarneus">Proxenus of Atarneus</a> became his guardian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHall201814_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHall201814-14">[12]</a></sup> Although little information about Aristotle\'s childhood has survived, he probably spent some time within the Macedonian palace, making his first connections with the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)#Kingship_and_the_royal_court" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonian monarchy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnagnostopoulos20134_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnagnostopoulos20134-15">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the age of seventeen or eighteen, Aristotle moved to Athens to continue his education at <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Plato\'s Academy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlits199958&#8211;63_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlits199958&#8211;63-16">[14]</a></sup> He remained there for nearly twenty years before leaving Athens in 348/47&#160;BC. The traditional story about his departure records that he was disappointed with the Academy\'s direction after control passed to Plato\'s nephew <a href="/wiki/Speusippus" title="Speusippus">Speusippus</a>, although it is possible that he feared the anti-Macedonian sentiments in Athens at that time and left before Plato died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle1984Introduction_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle1984Introduction-17">[15]</a></sup> Aristotle then accompanied <a href="/wiki/Xenocrates" title="Xenocrates">Xenocrates</a> to the court of his friend <a href="/wiki/Hermias_of_Atarneus" title="Hermias of Atarneus">Hermias of Atarneus</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. After the death of Hermias, Aristotle travelled with his pupil <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> to the island of <a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Lesbos</a>, where together they researched the <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a> and zoology of the island and its sheltered lagoon. While in Lesbos, Aristotle married <a href="/wiki/Pythias" title="Pythias">Pythias</a>, either Hermias\'s adoptive daughter or niece. She bore him a daughter, whom they also named Pythias. In 343 BC, Aristotle was invited by <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> to become the tutor to his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShields2016_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShields2016-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1972_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1972-6">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2241" data-file-width="1680" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg/170px-Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg/255px-Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg/340px-Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristoteles_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bust_(sculpture)" title="Bust (sculpture)">Portrait bust</a> of Aristotle; an <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Imperial Roman</a> (1st or 2nd century AD) copy of a lost <a href="/wiki/Bronze_sculpture" title="Bronze sculpture">bronze sculpture</a> made by <a href="/wiki/Lysippos" title="Lysippos">Lysippos</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle was appointed as the head of the royal academy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>. During Aristotle\'s time in the Macedonian court, he gave lessons not only to Alexander, but also to two other future kings: <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen199158&#8211;59_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen199158&#8211;59-19">[17]</a></sup> Aristotle encouraged Alexander toward eastern conquest and Aristotle\'s own attitude towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> was unabashedly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnocentricism" title="Ethnocentricism">ethnocentric</a>. In one famous example, he counsels Alexander to be "a leader to the Greeks and a despot to the barbarians, to look after the former as after friends and relatives, and to deal with the latter as with beasts or plants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen199158&#8211;59_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen199158&#8211;59-19">[17]</a></sup> By 335&#160;BC, Aristotle had returned to Athens, establishing his own school there known as the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_(Classical)" title="Lyceum (Classical)">Lyceum</a>. Aristotle conducted courses at the school for the next twelve years. While in Athens, his wife Pythias died and Aristotle became involved with <a href="/wiki/Herpyllis" title="Herpyllis">Herpyllis</a> of Stagira, who bore him a son whom he named after his father, <a href="/wiki/Nicomachus_(son_of_Aristotle)" title="Nicomachus (son of Aristotle)">Nicomachus</a>. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a></i>, he also had an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eromenos" title="Eromenos">er&#244;menos</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Palaephatus" title="Palaephatus"> Palaephatus of Abydus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2007_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2007-20">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This period in Athens, between 335 and 323 BC, is when Aristotle is believed to have composed many of his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1972_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1972-6">[5]</a></sup> He wrote many dialogues, of which only fragments have survived. Those works that have survived are in <a href="/wiki/Treatise" title="Treatise">treatise</a> form and were not, for the most part, intended for widespread publication; they are generally thought to be lecture aids for his students. His most important treatises include <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Soul" title="On the Soul">On the Soul</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Poetics_(Aristotle)" title="Poetics (Aristotle)">Poetics</a></i>. Aristotle studied and made significant contributions to "logic, metaphysics, mathematics, physics, biology, botany, ethics, politics, agriculture, medicine, dance and theatre."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(384&#8211;322_B.C.E.)_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(384&#8211;322_B.C.E.)-5">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Near the end of his life, Alexander and Aristotle became estranged over Alexander\'s relationship with Persia and Persians. A widespread tradition in antiquity suspected Aristotle of playing a role in Alexander\'s death, but the only evidence of this is an <a href="/wiki/Hagnothemis" title="Hagnothemis">unlikely claim</a> made some six years after the death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen1991460_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen1991460-21">[19]</a></sup> Following Alexander\'s death, anti-Macedonian sentiment in Athens was rekindled. In 322&#160;BC, Demophilus and <a href="/wiki/Eurymedon_the_Hierophant" title="Eurymedon the Hierophant">Eurymedon the Hierophant</a> reportedly denounced Aristotle for impiety,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFilonik201372&#8211;73_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFilonik201372&#8211;73-22">[20]</a></sup> prompting him to flee to his mother\'s family estate in <a href="/wiki/Chalcis" title="Chalcis"> Chalcis, on Euboea</a>, at which occasion he was said to have stated: "I will not allow the Athenians to sin twice against philosophy"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones1980216_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones1980216-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGigon201741_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGigon201741-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED&#252;ring1957T44a-e._25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED&#252;ring1957T44a-e.-25">[23]</a></sup> &#8211; a reference to Athens\'s <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Socrates" title="Trial of Socrates">trial and execution of Socrates</a>. He died on Euboea of natural causes later that same year, having named his student <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a> as his chief <a href="/wiki/Executor" title="Executor">executor</a> and leaving a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Will_(law)" title="Will (law)">will</a> in which he asked to be buried next to his wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase19923862_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase19923862-26">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abstract_philosophy">Abstract philosophy</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logic">Logic</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Term_logic" title="Term logic">Term logic</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Aristotelian_logic" title="Non-Aristotelian logic">Non-Aristotelian logic</a></div>\n<p>With the <i><a href="/wiki/Prior_Analytics" title="Prior Analytics">Prior Analytics</a></i>, Aristotle is credited with the earliest study of formal logic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDegnan199481&#8211;89_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDegnan199481&#8211;89-27">[25]</a></sup> and his conception of it was the dominant form of Western logic until 19th-century advances in <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECorcoran20091&#8211;20_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECorcoran20091&#8211;20-28">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a> stated in the <i><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Pure_Reason" title="Critique of Pure Reason">Critique of Pure Reason</a></i> that with Aristotle logic reached its completion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKant1787Preface_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKant1787Preface-29">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analytics_and_the_Organon">Analytics and the <i>Organon</i></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Organon</a></div>\n<table class="wikitable floatright">\n<caption>One of Aristotle\'s <a href="/wiki/Syllogism#Types" title="Syllogism">types of syllogism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[C]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>In words</th>\n<th>In terms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[D]</a></sup></th>\n<th>In equations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[E]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;All men are mortal.<br/><br/>&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;All Greeks are men.<br/><br/><a href="/wiki/Therefore_sign" title="Therefore sign">&#8756;</a> All Greeks are mortal.</td>\n<td>M a P<br/><br/>S a M<br/><br/>S a P</td>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg"><img alt="Modus Barbara Equations.svg" data-file-height="30" data-file-width="41" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg/180px-Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg/270px-Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg/360px-Modus_Barbara_Equations.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>What we today call <i>Aristotelian logic</i> with its <a href="/wiki/Syllogism#Types" title="Syllogism">types of syllogism</a> (methods of logical argument),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016-30">[28]</a></sup> Aristotle himself would have labelled "analytics". The term "logic" he reserved to mean <i>dialectics</i>. Most of Aristotle\'s work is probably not in its original form, because it was most likely edited by students and later lecturers. The logical works of Aristotle were compiled into a set of six books called the <i><a href="/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Organon</a></i> around 40 BC by <a href="/wiki/Andronicus_of_Rhodes" title="Andronicus of Rhodes">Andronicus of Rhodes</a> or others among his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPickover200952_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPickover200952-35">[30]</a></sup> The books are:\n</p>\n<ol><li><i>Categories</i></li>\n<li><i>On Interpretation</i></li>\n<li><i>Prior Analytics</i></li>\n<li><i>Posterior Analytics</i></li>\n<li><i>Topics</i></li>\n<li><i>On Sophistical Refutations</i></li></ol>\n<p>The order of the books (or the teachings from which they are composed) is not certain, but this list was derived from analysis of Aristotle\'s writings. It goes from the basics, the analysis of simple terms in the <i>Categories,</i> the analysis of propositions and their elementary relations in <i>On Interpretation</i>, to the study of more complex forms, namely, syllogisms (in the <i>Analytics</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrior_Analytics24b18&#8211;20_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrior_Analytics24b18&#8211;20-36">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBobzien2015_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBobzien2015-37">[32]</a></sup> and dialectics (in the <i>Topics</i> and <i>Sophistical Refutations</i>). The first three treatises form the core of the logical theory <i>stricto sensu</i>: the grammar of the language of logic and the correct rules of reasoning. The <i>Rhetoric</i> is not conventionally included, but it states that it relies on the <i>Topics</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2017_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2017-38">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="804" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/170px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/255px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/340px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> (left) and Aristotle in <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>\'s 1509 fresco, <i><a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i>. Aristotle holds his <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i> and gestures to the earth, representing empirical observation, whilst Plato gestures to the heavens, representing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Forms" title="The Forms">The Forms</a>, and holds his <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchool_of_Athens_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchool_of_Athens-39">[34]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Like his teacher Plato, Aristotle\'s philosophy aims at the <a href="/wiki/Universality_(philosophy)" title="Universality (philosophy)">universal</a>. Aristotle\'s <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a> places the universal (<i>katholou</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Particular" title="Particular">particulars</a> (<i>kath\' hekaston</i>), things in the world, whereas for Plato the universal is a separately existing form which actual things imitate. This means that Aristotle\'s <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> is based on the study of things that exist or happen in the world, and rises to knowledge of the universal, whereas for Plato epistemology begins with knowledge of universal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" title="Theory of Forms">Forms</a> (or ideas) and descends to knowledge of particular imitations of these.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2017_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2017-38">[33]</a></sup> For Aristotle, "form" is still what <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomena</a> are based on, but is "instantiated" in a particular substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2016_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2016-40">[35]</a></sup> Aristotle uses <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">induction</a> from examples alongside <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a>, whereas Plato relies on deduction from <i><a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a></i> principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2017_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2017-38">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Aristotle\'s terminology, "natural philosophy" is a branch of philosophy examining the phenomena of the natural world, and includes fields that would be regarded today as physics, biology and other natural sciences. Aristotle\'s work encompassed virtually all facets of intellectual inquiry. Aristotle makes philosophy in the broad sense coextensive with reasoning, which he also would describe as "science". Note, however, that his use of the term <i>science</i> carries a different meaning than that covered by the term "scientific method". For Aristotle, "all science (<i>dianoia</i>) is either practical, poetical or theoretical" (<i>Metaphysics</i> 1025b25). His practical science includes ethics and politics; his poetical science means the study of fine arts including poetry; his theoretical science covers physics, mathematics and metaphysics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016-41">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics (Aristotle)</a></div>\n<p>The word "metaphysics" appears to have been coined by the first century AD editor who assembled various small selections of Aristotle\'s works to the treatise we know by the name <i>Metaphysics</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000-42">[37]</a></sup> Aristotle called it "first philosophy", and distinguished it from mathematics and natural science (physics) as the contemplative (<i>theoretik&#275;</i>) philosophy which is "theological" and studies the divine. He wrote in his <i>Metaphysics</i> (1026a16):\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> if there were no other independent things besides the composite natural ones, the study of nature would be the primary kind of knowledge; but if there is some motionless independent thing, the knowledge of this precedes it and is first philosophy, and it is universal <i>in just this way</i>, because it is first. And it belongs to this sort of philosophy to study being as being, both what it is and what belongs to it just by virtue of being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle1999111_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle1999111-43">[38]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h4><span id="Substance.2C_potentiality_and_actuality"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Substance,_potentiality_and_actuality">Substance, potentiality and actuality</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">Hylomorphism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality_(Aristotle)" title="Potentiality and actuality (Aristotle)">Potentiality and actuality (Aristotle)</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle examines the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a> (<i>ousia</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> (<i>to ti &#234;n einai</i>, "the what it was to be") in his <i>Metaphysics</i> (Book VII), and he concludes that a particular substance is a combination of both matter and form, a philosophical theory called <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphism</a>. In Book VIII, he distinguishes the matter of the substance as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Material_substratum" title="Material substratum">substratum</a>, or the stuff of which it is composed. For example, the matter of a house is the bricks, stones, timbers etc., or whatever constitutes the <i>potential</i> house, while the form of the substance is the <i>actual</i> house, namely \'covering for bodies and chattels\' or any other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genus-differentia_definition" title="Genus-differentia definition">differentia</a> that let us define something as a house. The formula that gives the components is the account of the matter, and the formula that gives the differentia is the account of the form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1043a_10&#8211;30_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1043a_10&#8211;30-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2016_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2016-40">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With regard to the change (<i><a href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality" title="Potentiality and actuality">kinesis</a></i>) and its causes now, as he defines in his <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/On_Generation_and_Corruption" title="On Generation and Corruption">On Generation and Corruption</a></i> 319b&#8211;320a, he distinguishes the coming to be from:\n</p>\n<ol><li>growth and diminution, which is change in quantity;</li>\n<li>locomotion, which is change in space; and</li>\n<li>alteration, which is change in quality.</li></ol>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1698" data-file-width="2122" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg/220px-Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg/330px-Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg/440px-Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flute-player_dolphin_Alcesti_Group_MAN.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle argued that a capability like playing the flute could be acquired &#8211; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_actuality_(Aristotle)" title="Potentiality and actuality (Aristotle)">the potential made actual</a> &#8211; by learning.</div></div></div>\n<p>The coming to be is a change where nothing persists of which the resultant is a property. In that particular change he introduces the concept of potentiality (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunamis" title="Dunamis">dynamis</a></i>) and actuality (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entelecheia" title="Entelecheia">entelecheia</a></i>) in association with the matter and the form. Referring to potentiality, this is what a thing is capable of doing, or being acted upon, if the conditions are right and it is not prevented by something else. For example, the seed of a plant in the soil is potentially (<i>dynamei</i>) plant, and if it is not prevented by something, it will become a plant. Potentially beings can either \'act\' (<i>poiein</i>) or \'be acted upon\' (<i>paschein</i>), which can be either innate or learned. For example, the eyes possess the potentiality of sight (innate &#8211; being acted upon), while the capability of playing the flute can be possessed by learning (exercise &#8211; acting). Actuality is the fulfilment of the end of the potentiality. Because the end (<i>telos</i>) is the principle of every change, and for the sake of the end exists potentiality, therefore actuality is the end. Referring then to our previous example, we could say that an actuality is when a plant does one of the activities that plants do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2016_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2016-40">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> For that for the sake of which (<i>to hou heneka</i>) a thing is, is its principle, and the becoming is for the sake of the end; and the actuality is the end, and it is for the sake of this that the potentiality is acquired. For animals do not see in order that they may have sight, but they have sight that they may see.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsIX_1050a_5&#8211;10_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsIX_1050a_5&#8211;10-45">[40]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In summary, the matter used to make a house has potentiality to be a house and both the activity of building and the form of the final house are actualities, which is also a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Final_cause" title="Final cause">final cause</a> or end. Then Aristotle proceeds and concludes that the actuality is prior to potentiality in formula, in time and in substantiality. With this definition of the particular substance (i.e., matter and form), Aristotle tries to solve the problem of the unity of the beings, for example, "what is it that makes a man one"? Since, according to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> there are two Ideas: animal and biped, how then is man a unity? However, according to Aristotle, the potential being (matter) and the actual one (form) are one and the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2016_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2016-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1045a&#8211;b_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetaphysicsVIII_1045a&#8211;b-46">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Universals_and_particulars">Universals and particulars</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg/350px-Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg/525px-Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg/700px-Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Platonic_and_Aristotelian_Forms.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_forms" title="Theory of forms">forms</a> exist as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universals" title="Universals">universals</a>, like the ideal form of an apple. For Aristotle, both matter and form belong to the individual thing (<a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphism</a>).</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_theory_of_universals" title="Aristotle\'s theory of universals">Aristotle\'s theory of universals</a></div>\n<p>Plato argued that all things have a <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_forms" title="Theory of forms">universal form</a>, which could be either a property or a relation to other things. When we look at an apple, for example, we see an apple, and we can also analyse a form of an apple. In this distinction, there is a particular apple and a universal form of an apple. Moreover, we can place an apple next to a book, so that we can speak of both the book and apple as being next to each other. Plato argued that there are some universal forms that are not a part of particular things. For example, it is possible that there is no particular good in existence, but "good" is still a proper universal form. Aristotle disagreed with Plato on this point, arguing that all universals are instantiated at some period of time, and that there are no universals that are unattached to existing things. In addition, Aristotle disagreed with Plato about the location of universals. Where Plato spoke of the world of forms, a place where all universal forms subsist, Aristotle maintained that universals exist within each thing on which each universal is predicated. So, according to Aristotle, the form of apple exists within each apple, rather than in the world of the forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2016_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2016-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd196843&#8211;47_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd196843&#8211;47-47">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_philosophy">Natural philosophy</span></h2>\n<p>Aristotle\'s "natural philosophy" spans a wide range of natural phenomena including those now covered by physics, biology and other natural sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016-41">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="634" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg/220px-Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg/330px-Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg/440px-Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Four_Classical_Elements_in_Burning_Log.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The four <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical elements</a> (fire, air, water, earth) of <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> and Aristotle illustrated with a burning log. The log releases all four elements as it is destroyed.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Five_elements">Five elements</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">Classical element</a></div>\n<p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/On_Generation_and_Corruption" title="On Generation and Corruption">On Generation and Corruption</a></i>, Aristotle related each of the four elements proposed earlier by <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">Water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">Air</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">Fire</a>, to two of the four sensible qualities, hot, cold, wet, and dry. In the Empedoclean scheme, all matter was made of the four elements, in differing proportions. Aristotle\'s scheme added the heavenly <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">Aether</a>, the divine substance of the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">heavenly spheres</a>, stars and planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd1968133&#8211;39,_166&#8211;69_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd1968133&#8211;39,_166&#8211;69-48">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption><a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">Aristotle\'s elements</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd1968133&#8211;39,_166&#8211;69_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd1968133&#8211;39,_166&#8211;69-48">[43]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Element</th>\n<th><span style="color:red;">Hot</span>/<span style="color:blue;">Cold</span></th>\n<th><span style="color:green;">Wet</span>/<span style="color:brown;">Dry</span></th>\n<th>Motion</th>\n<th>Modern <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">state<br/>of matter</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">Earth</a></b></td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span></td>\n<td><span style="color:brown;">Dry</span></td>\n<td>Down</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">Solid</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">Water</a></b></td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span></td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Wet</span></td>\n<td>Down</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">Liquid</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">Air</a></b></td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Hot</span></td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Wet</span></td>\n<td>Up</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">Gas</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">Fire</a></b></td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Hot</span></td>\n<td><span style="color:brown;">Dry</span></td>\n<td>Up</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">Plasma</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">Aether</a></b></td>\n<td>(divine<br/>substance)</td>\n<td>&#8212;</td>\n<td>Circular<br/>(in heavens)</td>\n<td>&#8212;\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Motion">Motion</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_classical_mechanics" title="History of classical mechanics">History of classical mechanics</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle describes two kinds of motion: "violent" or "unnatural motion", such as that of a thrown stone, in the <i>Physics</i> (254b10), and "natural motion", such as of a falling object, in <i>On the Heavens</i> (300a20). In violent motion, as soon as the agent stops causing it, the motion stops also; in other words, the natural state of an object is to be at rest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllain2016_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllain2016-49">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[F]</a></sup> since Aristotle does not address <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup> With this understanding, it can be observed that, as Aristotle stated, heavy objects (on the ground, say) require more force to make them move; and objects pushed with greater force move faster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESusskind2011_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESusskind2011-52">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[G]</a></sup> This would imply the equation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESusskind2011_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESusskind2011-52">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle F=mv}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>F</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle F=mv}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle F=mv}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/11033403605ececd49fde3156e3914be4ac47f69" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.007ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>,</dd></dl></dd></dl>\n<p>incorrect in modern physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESusskind2011_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESusskind2011-52">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Natural motion depends on the element concerned: the aether naturally moves in a circle around the heavens,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[H]</a></sup> while the 4 Empedoclean elements move vertically up (like fire, as is observed) or down (like earth) towards their natural resting places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[I]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="755" data-file-width="825" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg/330px-Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg/495px-Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg/660px-Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_laws_of_motion.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle\'s laws of motion. In <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i> he states that objects fall at a speed proportional to their weight and inversely proportional to the density of the fluid they are immersed in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup> This is a correct approximation for objects in Earth\'s gravitational field moving in air or water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In the <i>Physics</i> (215a25), Aristotle effectively states a quantitative law, that the speed, v, of a falling body is proportional (say, with constant c) to its weight, W, and inversely proportional to the density,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[J]</a></sup> &#961;, of the fluid in which it is falling:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v=c{\\frac {W}{\\rho }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>W</mi>\n<mi>&#961;<!-- &#961; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v=c{\\frac {W}{\\rho }}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle v=c{\\frac {W}{\\rho }}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/63f55362fcfc38cda0b379cbc8a5df0c2c6367b3" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:8.504ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl></dd></dl>\n<p>Aristotle implies that in a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> the speed of fall would become infinite, and concludes from this apparent absurdity that a vacuum is not possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup> Opinions have varied on whether Aristotle intended to state quantitative laws. Henri Carteron held the "extreme view"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup> that Aristotle\'s concept of force was basically qualitative,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarteron19231&#8211;32_and_passim_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarteron19231&#8211;32_and_passim-58">[48]</a></sup> but other authors reject this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a> corrected Aristotle\'s theory that bodies move towards their natural resting places; metal boats can float if they <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle" title="Archimedes\' principle">displace enough water</a>; floating depends in Archimedes\' scheme on the mass and volume of the object, not as Aristotle thought its elementary composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle\'s writings on motion remained influential until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern" title="Early Modern">Early Modern</a> period. <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> (in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> are said to have shown by experiment that Aristotle\'s claim that a heavier object falls faster than a lighter object is incorrect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016-41">[36]</a></sup> A contrary opinion is given by <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Rovelli" title="Carlo Rovelli">Carlo Rovelli</a>, who argues that Aristotle\'s physics of motion is correct within its domain of validity, that of objects in the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>\'s gravitational field immersed in a fluid such as air. In this system, heavy bodies in steady fall indeed travel faster than light ones (whether friction is ignored, or not<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup>), and they do fall more slowly in a denser medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESusskind2011_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESusskind2011-52">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[K]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newton\'s "forced" motion corresponds to Aristotle\'s "violent" motion with its external agent, but Aristotle\'s assumption that the agent\'s effect stops immediately it stops acting (e.g., the ball leaves the thrower\'s hand) has awkward consequences: he has to suppose that surrounding fluid helps to push the ball along to make it continue to rise even though the hand is no longer acting on it, resulting in the Medieval <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">theory of impetus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Four_causes">Four causes</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Four_causes" title="Four causes">Four causes</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg/350px-Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg/525px-Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg/700px-Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_Four_Causes_of_a_Table.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle argued by analogy with woodwork that a thing takes its form from <a href="/wiki/Four_causes" title="Four causes">four causes</a>: in the case of a table, the wood used (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Material_cause" title="Material cause">material cause</a>), its design (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_cause" title="Formal cause">formal cause</a>), the tools and techniques used (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Efficient_cause" title="Efficient cause">efficient cause</a>), and its decorative or practical purpose (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Final_cause" title="Final cause">final cause</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi201588&#8211;90_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi201588&#8211;90-60">[49]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle suggested that the reason for anything coming about can be attributed to four different types of simultaneously active factors. His term <i>aitia</i> is traditionally translated as "cause", but it does not always refer to temporal sequence; it might be better translated as "explanation", but the traditional rendering will be employed here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07-61">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHankinson1998159_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHankinson1998159-62">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Material_cause" title="Material cause">Material cause</a> describes the material out of which something is composed. Thus the material cause of a table is wood. It is not about action. It does not mean that one domino knocks over another domino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07-61">[50]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_cause" title="Formal cause">formal cause</a> is its form, i.e., the arrangement of that matter. It tells us what a thing is, that a thing is determined by the definition, form, pattern, essence, whole, synthesis or archetype. It embraces the account of causes in terms of fundamental principles or general laws, as the whole (i.e., macrostructure) is the cause of its parts, a relationship known as the whole-part causation. Plainly put, the formal cause is the idea in the mind of the sculptor that brings the sculpture into being. A simple example of the formal cause is the mental image or idea that allows an artist, architect, or engineer to create a drawing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07-61">[50]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Efficient_cause" title="Efficient cause">efficient cause</a> is "the primary source", or that from which the change under consideration proceeds. It identifies \'what makes of what is made and what causes change of what is changed\' and so suggests all sorts of agents, nonliving or living, acting as the sources of change or movement or rest. Representing the current understanding of causality as the relation of cause and effect, this covers the modern definitions of "cause" as either the agent or agency or particular events or states of affairs. In the case of two dominoes, when the first is knocked over it causes the second also to fall over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07-61">[50]</a></sup> In the case of animals, this agency is a combination of <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">how it develops from the egg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">how its body functions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi201591&#8211;92,_369&#8211;73_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi201591&#8211;92,_369&#8211;73-63">[52]</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Final_cause" title="Final cause">final cause</a> (<i>telos</i>) is its purpose, the reason why a thing exists or is done, including both purposeful and instrumental actions and activities. The final cause is the purpose or function that something is supposed to serve. This covers modern ideas of motivating causes, such as volition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELloyd199696&#8211;100,_106&#8211;07-61">[50]</a></sup> In the case of living things, it implies <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a> to a particular way of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi201591&#8211;92,_369&#8211;73_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi201591&#8211;92,_369&#8211;73-63">[52]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics">Optics</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_optics" title="History of optics">History of optics</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle describes experiments in <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Camera_obscura" title="Camera obscura">camera obscura</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Problems_(Aristotle)" title="Problems (Aristotle)">Problems</a></i>, book 15. The apparatus consisted of a dark chamber with a small <a href="/wiki/Aperture" title="Aperture">aperture</a> that let light in. With it, he saw that whatever shape he made the hole, the sun\'s image always remained circular. He also noted that increasing the distance between the aperture and the image surface magnified the image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahanas_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahanas-64">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chance_and_spontaneity">Chance and spontaneity</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Accident_(philosophy)" title="Accident (philosophy)">Accident (philosophy)</a></div>\n<p>According to Aristotle, spontaneity and chance are causes of some things, distinguishable from other types of cause such as simple necessity. Chance as an incidental cause lies in the realm of <a href="/wiki/Accident_(philosophy)" title="Accident (philosophy)">accidental things</a>, "from what is spontaneous". There is also more a specific kind of chance, which Aristotle names "luck", that only applies to people\'s moral choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhysics2.6_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhysics2.6-65">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1973204&#8211;13_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1973204&#8211;13-66">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_astronomy" title="History of astronomy">History of astronomy</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, Aristotle refuted <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a>\'s claim that the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> was made up of "those stars which are shaded by the earth from the sun\'s rays," pointing out correctly that if "the size of the sun is greater than that of the earth and the distance of the stars from the earth many times greater than that of the sun, then... the sun shines on all the stars and the earth screens none of them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeteorology1._8_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeteorology1._8-67">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1230" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg/220px-DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg/330px-DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg/440px-DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DenglerSW-Stromboli-20040928-1230x800.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle noted that the ground level of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeolian_islands" title="Aeolian islands">Aeolian islands</a> changed before a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_eruption" title="Volcanic eruption">volcanic eruption</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_geology" title="History of geology">History of geology</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle was one of the first people to record any <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geological</a> observations. He stated that <a href="/wiki/Uniformitarianism" title="Uniformitarianism">geological change</a> was too slow to be observed in one person\'s lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore195613_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore195613-68">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeteorologyBook_1,_Part_14_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeteorologyBook_1,_Part_14-69">[58]</a></sup>\nThe geologist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a> noted that Aristotle described such change, including "lakes that had dried up" and "deserts that had become watered by rivers", giving as examples the growth of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_delta" title="Nile delta">Nile delta</a> since the time of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, and "the upheaving of one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeolian_islands" title="Aeolian islands">Aeolian islands</a>, previous to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_eruption" title="Volcanic eruption">volcanic eruption</a>."\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELyell183217_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELyell183217-70">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle\'s biology">Aristotle\'s biology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="606" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg/170px-Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg/255px-Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg/340px-Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tremoctopus_violaceus5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Among many pioneering zoological observations, Aristotle described the reproductive <a href="/wiki/Hectocotylus" title="Hectocotylus">hectocotyl arm</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a> (bottom left).</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empirical_research">Empirical research</span></h4>\n<p>Aristotle was the first person to study biology systematically,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi20157_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi20157-71">[60]</a></sup> and biology forms a large part of his writings. He spent two years observing and describing the zoology of <a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Lesbos</a> and the surrounding seas, including in particular the Pyrrha lagoon in the centre of Lesbos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi201514_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi201514-72">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson1910Prefatory_Note_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson1910Prefatory_Note-73">[62]</a></sup> His data in <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Animals" title="History of Animals">History of Animals</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Generation_of_Animals" title="Generation of Animals">Generation of Animals</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Movement_of_Animals" title="Movement of Animals">Movement of Animals</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Parts_of_Animals" title="Parts of Animals">Parts of Animals</a></i> are assembled from his own observations, statements given by people with specialised knowledge such as beekeepers and fishermen, and less accurate accounts provided by travellers from overseas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015196,_248_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015196,_248-74">[63]</a></sup> His apparent emphasis on animals rather than plants is a historical accident: his works on <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a> have been lost, but two books on plants by his pupil Theophrastus have survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDay20135805&#8211;16_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDay20135805&#8211;16-75">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle reports on the sea-life visible from observation on Lesbos and the catches of fishermen. He describes the <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_ray" title="Electric ray">electric ray</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frogfish" title="Frogfish">frogfish</a> in detail, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_nautilus" title="Paper nautilus">paper nautilus</a>. His description of the <a href="/wiki/Hectocotylus" title="Hectocotylus">hectocotyl arm</a> of cephalopods, used in sexual reproduction, was widely disbelieved until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi201566&#8211;74,_137_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi201566&#8211;74,_137-76">[65]</a></sup> He gives accurate descriptions of the four-chambered fore-stomachs of <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015118&#8211;19_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015118&#8211;19-77">[66]</a></sup> and of the <a href="/wiki/Ovoviviparity" title="Ovoviviparity">ovoviviparous</a> embryological development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hound_shark" title="Hound shark">hound shark</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi201573_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi201573-78">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He notes that an animal\'s structure is well matched to function, so, among birds, the <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">heron</a>, which lives in marshes with soft mud and lives by catching fish, has a long neck and long legs, and a sharp spear-like beak, whereas <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">ducks</a> that swim have short legs and webbed feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015135&#8211;36_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015135&#8211;36-79">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Darwin</a>, too, noted these sorts of differences between similar kinds of animal, but unlike Aristotle used the data to come to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015206_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015206-80">[69]</a></sup> Aristotle\'s writings can seem to modern readers close to implying evolution, but while Aristotle was aware that new mutations or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybridisation_(biology)" title="Hybridisation (biology)">hybridisations</a> could occur, he saw these as rare accidents. For Aristotle, accidents, like heat waves in winter, must be considered distinct from natural causes. He was thus critical of Empedocles\'s materialist theory of a "survival of the fittest" origin of living things and their organs, and ridiculed the idea that accidents could lead to orderly results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESedley2007189_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedley2007189-81">[70]</a></sup> To put his views into modern terms, he nowhere says that different species can have a <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common ancestor</a>, or that one kind can <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">change into another</a>, or that kinds can become <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015273_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015273-82">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_style">Scientific style</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="462" data-file-width="762" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg/310px-Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg/465px-Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg/620px-Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_of_Aristotle%27s_Growth_Laws.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle inferred growth laws from his observations on animals, including that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brood_size" title="Brood size">brood size</a> decreases with body mass, whereas <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a> period increases. He was correct in these predictions, at least for mammals: data are shown for mouse and elephant.</div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle did not do experiments in the modern sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor192242_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor192242-83">[72]</a></sup> He used the ancient Greek term <i>pepeiramenoi</i> to mean observations, or at most investigative procedures like dissection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015361&#8211;65_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015361&#8211;65-84">[73]</a></sup> In <i>Generation of Animals</i>, he finds a fertilised hen\'s egg of a suitable stage and opens it to see the embryo\'s heart beating inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2011_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2011-85">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015197&#8211;200_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015197&#8211;200-86">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Instead, he practised a different style of science: systematically gathering data, discovering patterns common to whole groups of animals, and inferring possible causal explanations from these.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015365&#8211;68_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015365&#8211;68-87">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor192249_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor192249-88">[77]</a></sup> This style is common in modern biology when large amounts of data become available in a new field, such as <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomics</a>. It does not result in the same certainty as experimental science, but it sets out testable hypotheses and constructs a narrative explanation of what is observed. In this sense, Aristotle\'s biology is scientific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015365&#8211;68_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015365&#8211;68-87">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nFrom the data he collected and documented, Aristotle inferred quite a number of <a href="/wiki/Biological_rules" title="Biological rules">rules</a> relating the life-history features of the live-bearing tetrapods (terrestrial placental mammals) that he studied. Among these correct predictions are the following. Brood size decreases with (adult) body mass, so that an elephant has fewer young (usually just one) per brood than a mouse. <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">Lifespan</a> increases with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestation_period" title="Gestation period">gestation period</a>, and also with body mass, so that elephants live longer than mice, have a longer period of gestation, and are heavier. As a final example, <a href="/wiki/Fecundity" title="Fecundity">fecundity</a> decreases with lifespan, so long-lived kinds like elephants have fewer young in total than short-lived kinds like mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015408_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015408-89">[78]</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_living_things">Classification of living things</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scala_naturae" title="Scala naturae">Scala naturae</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scyliorhinus_retifer_embryo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Scyliorhinus_retifer_embryo.JPG/220px-Scyliorhinus_retifer_embryo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Scyliorhinus_retifer_embryo.JPG/330px-Scyliorhinus_retifer_embryo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Scyliorhinus_retifer_embryo.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scyliorhinus_retifer_embryo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle recorded that the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mustelus_canis" title="Mustelus canis">a dogfish</a> was attached by a cord to a kind of placenta (the <a href="/wiki/Yolk_sac" title="Yolk sac">yolk sac</a>), like a higher animal; this formed an exception to the linear scale from highest to lowest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi201572&#8211;74_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi201572&#8211;74-90">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle distinguished about 500 species of <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergstromDugatkin201235_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergstromDugatkin201235-91">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhodes19747_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodes19747-92">[81]</a></sup> arranging these in the <i>History of Animals</i> in a graded scale of perfection, a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scala_naturae" title="Scala naturae">scala naturae</a></i>, with man at the top. His system had eleven grades of animal, from highest potential to lowest, expressed in their form at birth: the highest gave <a href="/wiki/Viviparity" title="Viviparity">live birth</a> to hot and wet creatures, the lowest laid cold, dry mineral-like eggs. Animals came above <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, and these in turn were above minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayr1982201&#8211;02_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayr1982201&#8211;02-93">[82]</a></sup> see also:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELovejoy1976_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovejoy1976-94">[83]</a></sup> He grouped what the modern zoologist would call <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> as the hotter "animals with blood", and below them the colder <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> as "animals without blood". Those with blood were divided into the live-bearing (<a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>), and the egg-laying (<a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>). Those without blood were insects, crustacea (non-shelled &#8211; cephalopods, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">shelled</a>) and the hard-shelled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a>). He recognised that animals did not exactly fit into a linear scale, and noted various exceptions, such as that sharks had a <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a> like the tetrapods. To a modern biologist, the explanation, not available to Aristotle, is <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015111&#8211;19_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015111&#8211;19-95">[84]</a></sup> He believed that purposive final causes guided all natural processes; this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teleological" title="Teleological">teleological</a> view justified his observed data as an expression of formal design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMason197943&#8211;44_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMason197943&#8211;44-96">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="font-size: 80%;">\n<caption>Aristotle\'s V</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:green;">Wet</span>, including eggs\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>(Among the egg-laying fishes):<br/>placental <a class="mw-redirect" (fish)">skate</a></td>\n<td>with&#160;blood</td>\n<td>none</td>\n<td>S, V</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:green;">Wet</span>, but <a href="/wiki/Placenta" style="color:green;">Wet</span> except shell\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalopods" title="Cephalopods">Cephalopods</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">Squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a></td>\n<td>without</td>\n<td>tentacles</td>\n<td>S, V</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:green;">Wet</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">Hard-shelled animals</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cockle_(bivalve)" title="Cockle (bivalve)">Cockle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charonia_variegata" title="Charonia variegata">trumpet snail</a></td>\n<td>without</td>\n<td>none</td>\n<td>S, V</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:brown;">Dry</span> (mineral shell)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Larva-bearing insects</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">Ant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">cicada</a></td>\n<td>without</td>\n<td>6 legs</td>\n<td>S, V</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:brown;">Dry</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">Spontaneously-generating</a></td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sponges" title="Sponges">Sponges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worm" title="Worm">worms</a></td>\n<td>without</td>\n<td>none</td>\n<td>S, V</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:green;">Wet</span> or<span style="color:brown;">Dry</span>, from earth\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">Plants</a></td>\n<td><a
