href="/wiki/Common_fig" title="Common fig">Fig</a></td>\n<td>without</td>\n<td>none</td>\n<td>V</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:brown;">Dry</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">Minerals</a></td>\n<td>Iron</td>\n<td>without</td>\n<td>none</td>\n<td>none</td>\n<td><span style="color:blue;">Cold</span>,<span style="color:brown;">Dry</span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soul">Soul</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/On_the_Soul" title="On the Soul">On the Soul</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotelian_Soul.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="913" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aristotelian_Soul.png/330px-Aristotelian_Soul.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aristotelian_Soul.png/495px-Aristotelian_Soul.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aristotelian_Soul.png/660px-Aristotelian_Soul.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotelian_Soul.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle proposed a three-part <a href="/wiki/Soul#Aristotle" title="Soul">structure for souls</a> of plants, animals, and humans, making humans unique in having all three types of soul.</div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle\'s <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, given in his treatise <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Soul" title="On the Soul">On the Soul</a></i> (<i>peri psych&#275;s</i>), posits three kinds of <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> ("psyches"): the vegetative soul, the sensitive soul, and the rational soul. Humans have a rational soul. The human soul incorporates the powers of the other kinds: Like the vegetative soul it can grow and nourish itself; like the sensitive soul it can experience sensations and move locally. The unique part of the human, rational soul is its ability to receive forms of other things and to compare them using the <i><a href="/wiki/Nous#Aristotle" title="Nous">nous</a></i> (intellect) and <i>logos</i> (reason).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015156&#8211;63_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015156&#8211;63-97">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For Aristotle, the soul is the <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism#Body&#8211;soul_hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">form</a> of a living being. Because all beings are composites of form and matter, the form of living beings is that which endows them with what is specific to living beings, e.g. the ability to initiate movement (or in the case of plants, growth and chemical transformations, which Aristotle considers types of movement).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShields2016_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShields2016-18">[16]</a></sup> In contrast to earlier philosophers, but in accordance with the Egyptians, he placed the rational soul in the heart, rather than the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMason197945_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMason197945-98">[87]</a></sup> Notable is Aristotle\'s division of sensation and thought, which generally differed from the concepts of previous philosophers, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Alcmaeon_of_Croton" title="Alcmaeon of Croton">Alcmaeon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie2010348_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie2010348-99">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Memory">Memory</span></h4>\n<p>According to Aristotle in <i>On the Soul</i>, memory is the ability to hold a perceived experience in the mind and to distinguish between the internal "appearance" and an occurrence in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch200712_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch200712-100">[89]</a></sup> In other words, a memory is a mental picture (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/phantasm" title="wikt:phantasm">phantasm</a>) that can be recovered. Aristotle believed an impression is left on a semi-fluid bodily organ that undergoes several changes in order to make a memory. A memory occurs when <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(psychology)" title="Stimulus (psychology)">stimuli</a> such as sights or sounds are so complex that the nervous system cannot receive all the impressions at once. These changes are the same as those involved in the operations of sensation, Aristotelian \'<a href="/wiki/Common_sense" title="Common sense">common sense</a>\', and thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch200761_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch200761-101">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarruthers200716_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarruthers200716-102">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle uses the term \'memory\' for the actual retaining of an experience in the impression that can develop from sensation, and for the intellectual anxiety that comes with the impression because it is formed at a particular time and processing specific contents. Memory is of the past, prediction is of the future, and sensation is of the present. Retrieval of impressions cannot be performed suddenly. A transitional channel is needed and located in our past experiences, both for our previous experience and present experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch200725_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch200725-103">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because Aristotle believes people receive all kinds of sense perceptions and perceive them as impressions, people are continually weaving together new impressions of experiences. To search for these impressions, people search the memory itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren192130_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren192130-104">[93]</a></sup> Within the memory, if one experience is offered instead of a specific memory, that person will reject this experience until they find what they are looking for. Recollection occurs when one retrieved experience naturally follows another. If the chain of "images" is needed, one memory will stimulate the next. When people recall experiences, they stimulate certain previous experiences until they reach the one that is needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren192125_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren192125-105">[94]</a></sup> Recollection is thus the self-directed activity of retrieving the information stored in a memory impression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarruthers200719_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarruthers200719-106">[95]</a></sup> Only humans can remember impressions of intellectual activity, such as numbers and words. Animals that have perception of time can retrieve memories of their past observations. Remembering involves only perception of the things remembered and of the time passed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1921296_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1921296-107">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:512px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="361" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg/510px-Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg/765px-Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg/1020px-Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg.png 2x" width="510"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Senses_Perception_Memory_Dreams_Action.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Senses, perception, memory, dreams, action in Aristotle\'s psychology. Impressions are stored in the <a href="/wiki/Sensorium" title="Sensorium">seat of perception</a>, linked by his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laws_of_Association" title="Laws of Association">Laws of Association</a> (similarity, contrast, and <a href="/wiki/Contiguity_(psychology)" title="Contiguity (psychology)">contiguity</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle believed the chain of thought, which ends in recollection of certain impressions, was connected systematically in relationships such as similarity, contrast, and <a href="/wiki/Contiguity_(psychology)" title="Contiguity (psychology)">contiguity</a>, described in his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laws_of_Association" title="Laws of Association">Laws of Association</a>. Aristotle believed that past experiences are hidden within the mind. A force operates to awaken the hidden material to bring up the actual experience. According to Aristotle, association is the power innate in a mental state, which operates upon the unexpressed remains of former experiences, allowing them to rise and be recalled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarren1921259_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarren1921259-108">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dreams">Dreams</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dream#Classical_history" title="Dream">Dream &#167;&#160;Classical history</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle describes sleep in <i>On Sleep and Wakefulness</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23-109">[98]</a></sup> Sleep takes place as a result of overuse of the senses<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18-110">[99]</a></sup> or of digestion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23-109">[98]</a></sup> so it is vital to the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18-110">[99]</a></sup> While a person is asleep, the critical activities, which include thinking, sensing, recalling and remembering, do not function as they do during wakefulness. Since a person cannot sense during sleep they can not have desire, which is the result of sensation. However, the senses are able to work during sleep,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18-110">[99]</a></sup> albeit differently,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23-109">[98]</a></sup> unless they are weary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18_110-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18-110">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dreams do not involve actually sensing a stimulus. In dreams, sensation is still involved, but in an altered manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18_110-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18-110">[99]</a></sup> Aristotle explains that when a person stares at a moving stimulus such as the waves in a body of water, and then look away, the next thing they look at appears to have a wavelike motion. When a person perceives a stimulus and the stimulus is no longer the focus of their attention, it leaves an impression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23_109-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23-109">[98]</a></sup> When the body is awake and the senses are functioning properly, a person constantly encounters new stimuli to sense and so the impressions of previously perceived stimuli are ignored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18_110-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShute1941115&#8211;18-110">[99]</a></sup> However, during sleep the impressions made throughout the day are noticed as there are no new distracting sensory experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23_109-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23-109">[98]</a></sup> So, dreams result from these lasting impressions. Since impressions are all that are left and not the exact stimuli, dreams do not resemble the actual waking experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEModrak2009169&#8211;81_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEModrak2009169&#8211;81-111">[100]</a></sup> During sleep, a person is in an altered state of mind. Aristotle compares a sleeping person to a person who is overtaken by strong feelings toward a stimulus. For example, a person who has a strong infatuation with someone may begin to think they see that person everywhere because they are so overtaken by their feelings. Since a person sleeping is in a suggestible state and unable to make judgements, they become easily deceived by what appears in their dreams, like the infatuated person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23_109-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23-109">[98]</a></sup> This leads the person to believe the dream is real, even when the dreams are absurd in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23_109-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolowchak1996405&#8211;23-109">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One component of Aristotle\'s theory of dreams disagrees with previously held beliefs. He claimed that dreams are not foretelling and not sent by a divine being. Aristotle reasoned naturalistically that instances in which dreams do resemble future events are simply coincidences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWebb1990174&#8211;84_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebb1990174&#8211;84-112">[101]</a></sup> Aristotle claimed that a dream is first established by the fact that the person is asleep when they experience it. If a person had an image appear for a moment after waking up or if they see something in the dark it is not considered a dream because they were awake when it occurred. Secondly, any sensory experience that is perceived while a person is asleep does not qualify as part of a dream. For example, if, while a person is sleeping, a door shuts and in their dream they hear a door is shut, this sensory experience is not part of the dream. Lastly, the images of dreams must be a result of lasting impressions of waking sensory experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEModrak2009169&#8211;81_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEModrak2009169&#8211;81-111">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_philosophy">Practical philosophy</span></h2>\n<p>Aristotle\'s practical philosophy covers areas such as ethics, politics, economics, and rhetoric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWildberg2016-41">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table align="right" class="wikitable floatright" style="font-size: 80%;">\n<caption>Virtues and their accompanying vices<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(384&#8211;322_B.C.E.)_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(384&#8211;322_B.C.E.)-5">[4]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Too little</th>\n<th>Virtuous mean</th>\n<th>Too much\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Humbleness</td>\n<td>High-mindedness</td>\n<td>Vainglory\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Lack of purpose</td>\n<td>Right ambition</td>\n<td>Over-ambition\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Spiritlessness</td>\n<td>Good temper</td>\n<td>Irascibility\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Rudeness</td>\n<td>Civility</td>\n<td>Obsequiousness\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Cowardice</td>\n<td>Courage</td>\n<td>Rashness\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Insensibility</td>\n<td>Self-control</td>\n<td>Intemperance\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sarcasm</td>\n<td>Sincerity</td>\n<td>Boastfulness\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Boorishness</td>\n<td>Wit</td>\n<td>Buffoonery\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Shamelessness</td>\n<td>Modesty</td>\n<td>Shyness\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Callousness</td>\n<td>Just resentment</td>\n<td>Spitefulness\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Pettiness</td>\n<td>Generosity</td>\n<td>Vulgarity\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Meanness</td>\n<td>Liberality</td>\n<td>Wastefulness\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Just_war_theory">Just war theory</span></h3>\n<p>Aristotelian <a href="/wiki/Just_war_theory" title="Just war theory">just war theory</a> is not well regarded in the present day, especially his view that warfare was justified to ensalve "natural slaves". In Aristotelian philosophy, the abolition of what he considers "<a href="/wiki/Natural_slavery" title="Natural slavery">natural slavery</a>" would undermine civic <a href="/wiki/Freedom" title="Freedom">freedom</a>. The pusuit of freedom is inseperable from pursuing mastery over "those who deserve to be slaves". According to <i>The Cambridge Companion to Aristotle\'s Politics</i> the targets of this aggresive warfare were non-Greeks, noting Aristotle\'s view that "our poets say \'it is proper for Greeks to rule non-Greeks\'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeslauriersDestr&#233;e2013157-162_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeslauriersDestr&#233;e2013157-162-113">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle generally has a favorable opinion of war, extolling it as a chance for <a href="/wiki/Virtue" title="Virtue">virtue</a> and writing that "the leisure that accompanies peace" tends to make people "arrogant". War to "avoid becoming enslaved to others" is justified as self-defense. He writes that war "compels people to be just and temperate", however, in order to be just "war must be chosen for the sake of peace" (with the exception of wars of aggression discussed above).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeslauriersDestr&#233;e2013157-162_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeslauriersDestr&#233;e2013157-162-113">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_ethics" title="Aristotelian ethics">Aristotelian ethics</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle considered ethics to be a practical rather than theoretical study, i.e., one aimed at becoming good and doing good rather than knowing for its own sake. He wrote several treatises on ethics, including most notably, the <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKraut2001_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKraut2001-114">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle taught that virtue has to do with the proper function (<i>ergon</i>) of a thing. An eye is only a good eye in so much as it can see, because the proper function of an eye is sight. Aristotle reasoned that humans must have a function specific to humans, and that this function must be an activity of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Anima" title="De Anima">psuch&#275;</a></i> (<i>soul</i>) in accordance with reason (<i><a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a></i>). Aristotle identified such an optimum activity (the virtuous mean, between the accompanying vices of excess or deficiency<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(384&#8211;322_B.C.E.)_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(384&#8211;322_B.C.E.)-5">[4]</a></sup>) of the soul as the aim of all human deliberate action, <i><a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a></i>, generally translated as "happiness" or sometimes "well being". To have the potential of ever being happy in this way necessarily requires a good character (<i>&#275;thik&#275;</i> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arete_(moral_virtue)" title="Arete (moral virtue)">aret&#275;</a></i>), often translated as moral or ethical virtue or excellence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicomachean_Ethics_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicomachean_Ethics-115">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle taught that to achieve a virtuous and potentially happy character requires a first stage of having the fortune to be habituated not deliberately, but by teachers, and experience, leading to a later stage in which one consciously chooses to do the best things. When the best people come to live life this way their practical wisdom (<i><a href="/wiki/Phronesis" title="Phronesis">phronesis</a></i>) and their intellect (<i><a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">nous</a></i>) can develop with each other towards the highest possible human virtue, the wisdom of an accomplished theoretical or speculative thinker, or in other words, a philosopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicomachean_EthicsBook_VI_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicomachean_EthicsBook_VI-116">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics (Aristotle)</a></div>\n<p>In addition to his works on ethics, which address the individual, Aristotle addressed the city in his work titled <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i>. Aristotle considered the city to be a natural community. Moreover, he considered the city to be prior in importance to the family which in turn is prior to the individual, "for the whole must of necessity be prior to the part".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPolitics1253a19&#8211;24_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPolitics1253a19&#8211;24-117">[106]</a></sup> He also famously stated that "man is by nature a political animal" and also arguing that humanity\'s defining factor among others in the animal kingdom is its rationality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle2009320&#8211;21_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle2009320&#8211;21-118">[107]</a></sup> Aristotle conceived of politics as being like an organism rather than like a machine, and as a collection of parts none of which can exist without the others. Aristotle\'s conception of the city is organic, and he is considered one of the first to conceive of the city in this manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbensteinEbenstein200259_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbensteinEbenstein200259-119">[108]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1189" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg/370px-Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg/555px-Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg/740px-Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg.png 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_constitutions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle\'s classifications of political constitutions</div></div></div>\n<p>The common modern understanding of a political community as a modern state is quite different from Aristotle\'s understanding. Although he was aware of the existence and potential of larger empires, the natural community according to Aristotle was the city (<i><a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">polis</a></i>) which functions as a political "community" or "partnership" (<i>koin&#333;nia</i>). The aim of the city is not just to avoid injustice or for economic stability, but rather to allow at least some citizens the possibility to live a good life, and to perform beautiful acts: "The political partnership must be regarded, therefore, as being for the sake of noble actions, not for the sake of living together." This is distinguished from modern approaches, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> theory, according to which individuals leave the <a href="/wiki/State_of_nature" title="State of nature">state of nature</a> because of "fear of violent death" or its "inconveniences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[L]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Protrepticus_(Aristotle)" title="Protrepticus (Aristotle)">Protrepticus</a></i>, the character \'Aristotle\' states:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchinsonJohnson201522_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchinsonJohnson201522-121">[109]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> For we all agree that the most excellent man should rule, i.e., the supreme by nature, and that the law rules and alone is authoritative; but the law is a kind of intelligence, i.e. a discourse based on intelligence. And again, what standard do we have, what criterion of good things, that is more precise than the intelligent man? For all that this man will choose, if the choice is based on his knowledge, are good things and their contraries are bad. And since everybody chooses most of all what conforms to their own proper dispositions (a just man choosing to live justly, a man with bravery to live bravely, likewise a self-controlled man to live with self-control), it is clear that the intelligent man will choose most of all to be intelligent; for this is the function of that capacity. Hence it\'s evident that, according to the most authoritative judgment, intelligence is supreme among goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHutchinsonJohnson201522_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHutchinsonJohnson201522-121">[109]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics (Aristotle)</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle made substantial contributions to <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economic thought</a>, especially to thought in the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins200020&#8211;24_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins200020&#8211;24-122">[110]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i>, Aristotle addresses the city, <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>. His response to criticisms of <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Robbins" title="Lionel Robbins">Lionel Robbins</a>\'s view, anticipated later proponents of private property among philosophers and economists, as it related to the overall <a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a> of social arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins200020&#8211;24_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins200020&#8211;24-122">[110]</a></sup> Aristotle believed that although communal arrangements may seem beneficial to society, and that although private property is often blamed for social strife, such evils in fact come from <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>. In <i>Politics</i>, Aristotle offers one of the earliest accounts of the origin of <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">money</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins200020&#8211;24_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins200020&#8211;24-122">[110]</a></sup> Money came into use because people became dependent on one another, importing what they needed and exporting the surplus. For the sake of convenience, people then agreed to deal in something that is intrinsically useful and easily applicable, such as iron or <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28-123">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle\'s discussions on <a href="/wiki/Retail" title="Retail">retail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interest" title="Interest">interest</a> was a major influence on economic thought in the Middle Ages. He had a low opinion of retail, believing that contrary to using money to procure things one needs in managing the household, retail trade seeks to make a <a href="/wiki/Profit_(economics)" title="Profit (economics)">profit</a>. It thus uses goods as a means to an end, rather than as an end unto itself. He believed that retail trade was in this way unnatural. Similarly, Aristotle considered making a profit through interest unnatural, as it makes a gain out of the money itself, and not from its use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28-123">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle gave a summary of the function of money that was perhaps remarkably precocious for his time. He wrote that because it is impossible to determine the value of every good through a count of the number of other goods it is worth, the necessity arises of a single universal standard of measurement. Money thus allows for the association of different goods and makes them "commensurable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28-123">[111]</a></sup> He goes to on state that money is also useful for future exchange, making it a sort of security. That is, "if we do not want a thing now, we shall be able to get it when we do want it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28_123-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle194816&#8211;28-123">[111]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rhetoric_and_poetics">Rhetoric and poetics</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux,_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3667" data-file-width="4803" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux%2C_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg/290px-B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux%2C_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux%2C_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg/435px-B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux%2C_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux%2C_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg/580px-B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux%2C_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux,_The_Blind_Oedipus_Commending_his_Children_to_the_Gods.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Blind Oedipus Commending his Children to the Gods</i> (1784) by <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9nigne_Gagneraux" title="B&#233;nigne Gagneraux">B&#233;nigne Gagneraux</a>. In his <i><a href="/wiki/Poetics_(Aristotle)" title="Poetics (Aristotle)">Poetics</a></i>, Aristotle uses the tragedy <i><a href="/wiki/Oedipus_Rex" title="Oedipus Rex">Oedipus Tyrannus</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a> as an example of how the perfect tragedy should be structured, with a generally good protagonist who starts the play prosperous, but loses everything through some <i><a href="/wiki/Hamartia" title="Hamartia">hamartia</a></i> (fault).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmann196856&#8211;60_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmann196856&#8211;60-124">[112]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric_(Aristotle)" title="Rhetoric (Aristotle)">Rhetoric (Aristotle)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poetics_(Aristotle)" title="Poetics (Aristotle)">Poetics (Aristotle)</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle\'s <i>Rhetoric</i> proposes that a speaker can use three basic kinds of appeals to persuade his audience: <i><a href="/wiki/Ethos" title="Ethos">ethos</a></i> (an appeal to the speaker\'s character), <i><a href="/wiki/Pathos" title="Pathos">pathos</a></i> (an appeal to the audience\'s emotion), and <i><a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a></i> (an appeal to logical reasoning).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarver1994109&#8211;10_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarver1994109&#8211;10-125">[113]</a></sup> He also categorises rhetoric into three genres: <a href="/wiki/Epideictic" title="Epideictic">epideictic</a> (ceremonial speeches dealing with praise or blame), <a href="/wiki/Forensic_rhetoric" title="Forensic rhetoric">forensic</a> (judicial speeches over guilt or innocence), and <a href="/wiki/Deliberative_rhetoric" title="Deliberative rhetoric">deliberative</a> (speeches calling on an audience to make a decision on an issue).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERorty19963&#8211;7_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERorty19963&#8211;7-126">[114]</a></sup> Aristotle also outlines two kinds of rhetorical <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof (truth)">proofs</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Enthymeme" title="Enthymeme">enthymeme</a></i> (proof by <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Paradeigma" title="Paradeigma">paradeigma</a></i> (proof by example).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimaldi199871_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimaldi199871-127">[115]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aristotle writes in his <i>Poetics</i> that <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poetry</a>, tragedy, comedy, <a href="/wiki/Dithyramb" title="Dithyramb"> dithyrambic poetry</a>, painting, sculpture, music, and dance are all fundamentally acts of <i><a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">mimesis</a></i> ("imitation"), each varying in imitation by medium, object, and manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59-128">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoeticsI_1447a_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoeticsI_1447a-129">[117]</a></sup> He applies the term <i>mimesis</i> both as a property of a work of art and also as the product of the artist\'s intention<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59-128">[116]</a></sup> and contends that the audience\'s realisation of the <i>mimesis</i> is vital to understanding the work itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59-128">[116]</a></sup> Aristotle states that <i>mimesis</i> is a natural instinct of humanity that separates humans from animals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59-128">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoeticsIV_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoeticsIV-130">[118]</a></sup> and that all human artistry "follows the pattern of nature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59-128">[116]</a></sup> Because of this, Aristotle believed that each of the mimetic arts possesses what <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Halliwell_(academic)" title="Stephen Halliwell (academic)">Stephen Halliwell</a> calls "highly structured procedures for the achievement of their purposes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59_128-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliwell2002152&#8211;59-128">[116]</a></sup> For example, music imitates with the media of rhythm and harmony, whereas dance imitates with rhythm alone, and poetry with language. The forms also differ in their object of imitation. Comedy, for instance, is a dramatic imitation of men worse than average; whereas tragedy imitates men slightly better than average. Lastly, the forms differ in their manner of imitation &#8211; through narrative or character, through change or no change, and through drama or no drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoeticsIII_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoeticsIII-131">[119]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While it is believed that Aristotle\'s <i>Poetics</i> originally comprised two books &#8211; one on comedy and one on tragedy &#8211; only the portion that focuses on tragedy has survived. Aristotle taught that tragedy is composed of six elements: plot-structure, character, style, thought, spectacle, and lyric poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoeticsVI_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoeticsVI-132">[120]</a></sup> The characters in a tragedy are merely a means of driving the story; and the plot, not the characters, is the chief focus of tragedy. Tragedy is the imitation of action arousing pity and fear, and is meant to effect the <a href="/wiki/Catharsis" title="Catharsis">catharsis</a> of those same emotions. Aristotle concludes <i>Poetics</i> with a discussion on which, if either, is superior: epic or tragic mimesis. He suggests that because tragedy possesses all the attributes of an epic, possibly possesses additional attributes such as spectacle and music, is more unified, and achieves the aim of its mimesis in shorter scope, it can be considered superior to epic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoeticsXXVI_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoeticsXXVI-133">[121]</a></sup> Aristotle was a keen systematic collector of riddles, folklore, and proverbs; he and his school had a special interest in the riddles of the <a href="/wiki/Pythia" title="Pythia">Delphic Oracle</a> and studied the fables of <a href="/wiki/Aesop" title="Aesop">Aesop</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAesop1998Introduction,_xi&#8211;xii_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAesop1998Introduction,_xi&#8211;xii-134">[122]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_on_women">Views on women</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_views_on_women" title="Aristotle\'s views on women">Aristotle\'s views on women</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology#Inheritance" title="Aristotle\'s biology">Aristotle\'s biology &#167;&#160;Inheritance</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle\'s analysis of procreation describes an active, ensouling masculine element bringing life to an inert, passive female element. On this ground, proponents of <a href="/wiki/Feminist_metaphysics" title="Feminist metaphysics">feminist metaphysics</a> have accused Aristotle of <a href="/wiki/Misogyny" title="Misogyny">misogyny</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeland1998_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeland1998-135">[123]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">sexism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorsink197983&#8211;112_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorsink197983&#8211;112-136">[124]</a></sup> However, Aristotle gave equal weight to women\'s happiness as he did to men\'s, and commented in his <i>Rhetoric</i> that the things that lead to happiness need to be in women as well as men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[M]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_writers_influenced_by_Aristotle" title="List of writers influenced by Aristotle">List of writers influenced by Aristotle</a></div>\n<p>More than 2300 years after his death, Aristotle remains one of the most influential people who ever lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi20158_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi20158-139">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle\'s_Influence2018_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle\'s_Influence2018-140">[127]</a></sup> He contributed to almost every field of human knowledge then in existence, and he was the founder of many new fields. According to the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Magee" title="Bryan Magee">Bryan Magee</a>, "it is doubtful whether any human being has ever known as much as he did".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagee201034_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagee201034-141">[128]</a></sup> Among countless other achievements, Aristotle was the founder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_logic" title="Formal logic">formal logic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1990156_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1990156-142">[129]</a></sup> pioneered the study of <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, and left every future scientist and philosopher in his debt through his contributions to the scientific method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(Greek_philosopher)_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle_(Greek_philosopher)-143">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurant200692_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurant200692-144">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77-145">[132]</a></sup> Taneli Kukkonen, writing in <i>The Classical Tradition</i>, observes that his achievement in founding two sciences is unmatched, and his reach in influencing "every branch of intellectual enterprise" including Western ethical and political theory, theology, rhetoric and literary analysis is equally long. As a result, Kukkonen argues, any analysis of reality today "will almost certainly carry Aristotelian overtones ... evidence of an exceptionally forceful mind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77-145">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Barnes" title="Jonathan Barnes">Jonathan Barnes</a> wrote that "an account of Aristotle\'s intellectual afterlife would be little less than a history of European thought".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnes198286_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnes198286-146">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="On_his_successor.2C_Theophrastus"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="On_his_successor,_Theophrastus">On his successor, Theophrastus</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historia_Plantarum_(Theophrastus)" title="Historia Plantarum (Theophrastus)">Historia Plantarum (Theophrastus)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="693" data-file-width="437" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg/170px-161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg/255px-161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg/340px-161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:161Theophrastus_161_frontespizio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Book_frontispiece" title="Book frontispiece">Frontispiece</a> to a 1644 version of <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Plantarum_(Theophrastus)" title="Historia Plantarum (Theophrastus)">Historia Plantarum</a></i>, originally written around 300 BC</div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle\'s pupil and successor, <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a>, wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Plantarum_(Theophrastus)" title="Historia Plantarum (Theophrastus)">History of Plants</a></i>, a pioneering work in botany. Some of his technical terms remain in use, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpel" title="Carpel">carpel</a> from <i>carpos</i>, fruit, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericarp" title="Pericarp">pericarp</a>, from <i>pericarpion</i>, seed chamber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooker1831219_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooker1831219-147">[134]</a></sup>\nTheophrastus was much less concerned with formal causes than Aristotle was, instead pragmatically describing how plants functioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayr198290&#8211;91_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayr198290&#8211;91-148">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMason197946_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMason197946-149">[136]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_later_Greek_philosophers">On later Greek philosophers</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a></div>\n<p>The immediate influence of Aristotle\'s work was felt as the Lyceum grew into the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a>. Aristotle\'s notable students included <a href="/wiki/Aristoxenus" title="Aristoxenus">Aristoxenus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">Dicaearchus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_of_Phalerum" title="Demetrius of Phalerum">Demetrius of Phalerum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eudemos_of_Rhodes" title="Eudemos of Rhodes">Eudemos of Rhodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harpalus" title="Harpalus">Harpalus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mnason_of_Phocis" title="Mnason of Phocis">Mnason of Phocis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicomachus_(son_of_Aristotle)" title="Nicomachus (son of Aristotle)">Nicomachus</a>, and Theophrastus. Aristotle\'s influence over Alexander the Great is seen in the latter\'s bringing with him on his expedition a host of zoologists, botanists, and researchers. He had also learned a great deal about Persian customs and traditions from his teacher. Although his respect for Aristotle was diminished as his travels made it clear that much of Aristotle\'s geography was clearly wrong, when the old philosopher released his works to the public, Alexander complained "Thou hast not done well to publish thy acroamatic doctrines; for in what shall I surpass other men if those doctrines wherein I have been trained are to be all men\'s common property?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919Part_1,_7:7_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919Part_1,_7:7-150">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Hellenistic_science">On Hellenistic science</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">Ancient Greek medicine</a></div>\n<p>After Theophrastus, the Lyceum failed to produce any original work. Though interest in Aristotle\'s ideas survived, they were generally taken unquestioningly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnnas2001252_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnnas2001252-151">[138]</a></sup> It is not until the age of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemies</a> that advances in biology can be again found.\n</p><p>The first medical teacher at Alexandria, <a href="/wiki/Herophilos" title="Herophilos">Herophilus of Chalcedon</a>, corrected Aristotle, placing intelligence in the brain, and connected the nervous system to motion and sensation. Herophilus also distinguished between <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a>, noting that the latter <a href="/wiki/Pulse" title="Pulse">pulse</a> while the former do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMason197956_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMason197956-152">[139]</a></sup> Though a few ancient <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a> challenged the <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleological</a> viewpoint of Aristotelian ideas about life, teleology (and after the rise of Christianity, <a href="/wiki/Natural_theology" title="Natural theology">natural theology</a>) would remain central to biological thought essentially until the 18th and 19th centuries. <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a> states that there was "nothing of any real consequence in biology after Lucretius and Galen until the Renaissance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayr198590&#8211;94_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayr198590&#8211;94-153">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Byzantine_scholars">On Byzantine scholars</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Commentaries_on_Aristotle" title="Commentaries on Aristotle">Commentaries on Aristotle</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Aristotelianism" title="Byzantine Aristotelianism">Byzantine Aristotelianism</a></div>\n<p>Greek Christian scribes played a crucial role in the preservation of Aristotle by copying all the extant Greek language manuscripts of the corpus. The first Greek Christians to comment extensively on Aristotle were Philoponus, Elias, and David in the sixth century, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stephen_of_Alexandria" title="Stephen of Alexandria">Stephen of Alexandria</a> in the early seventh century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorabji199020,_28,_35&#8211;36_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorabji199020,_28,_35&#8211;36-154">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> stands out for having attempted a fundamental critique of Aristotle\'s views on the eternity of the world, movement, and other elements of Aristotelian thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorabji1990233&#8211;74_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorabji1990233&#8211;74-155">[142]</a></sup> Philoponus questioned Aristotle\'s teaching of physics, noting its flaws and introducing the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">theory of impetus</a> to explain his observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindberg1992162_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindberg1992162-156">[143]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a hiatus of several centuries, formal commentary by Eustratius and <a href="/wiki/Michael_of_Ephesus" title="Michael of Ephesus">Michael of Ephesus</a> reappeared in the late eleventh and early twelfth centuries, apparently sponsored by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anna_Comnena" title="Anna Comnena">Anna Comnena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESorabji199020&#8211;21,_28&#8211;29,_393&#8211;406,_407&#8211;08_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESorabji199020&#8211;21,_28&#8211;29,_393&#8211;406,_407&#8211;08-157">[144]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_medieval_Islamic_world">On the medieval Islamic world</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_Islamic_philosophy" title="Logic in Islamic philosophy">Logic in Islamic philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transmission_of_the_Greek_Classics" title="Transmission of the Greek Classics">Transmission of the Greek Classics</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabic_aristotle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1563" data-file-width="1064" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Arabic_aristotle.jpg/170px-Arabic_aristotle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Arabic_aristotle.jpg/255px-Arabic_aristotle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Arabic_aristotle.jpg/340px-Arabic_aristotle.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabic_aristotle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Islamic portrayal of Aristotle, c. 1220</div></div></div>\n<p>Aristotle was one of the most revered Western thinkers in early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_theology" title="Islamic theology">Islamic theology</a>. Most of the still extant works of Aristotle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy-Day1998_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy-Day1998-158">[145]</a></sup> as well as a number of the original Greek commentaries, were translated into Arabic and studied by Muslim philosophers, scientists and scholars. <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpharabius" title="Alpharabius">Alpharabius</a>, who wrote on Aristotle in great depth, also influenced <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> and other Western Christian scholastic philosophers. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkindus" title="Alkindus">Alkindus</a> greatly admired Aristotle\'s philosophy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaley1989_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaley1989-159">[146]</a></sup> and Averroes spoke of Aristotle as the "exemplar" for all future philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAverroes1953III,_2,_43_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAverroes1953III,_2,_43-160">[147]</a></sup> Medieval Muslim scholars regularly described Aristotle as the "First Teacher".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy-Day1998_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy-Day1998-158">[145]</a></sup> The title "teacher" was first given to Aristotle by Muslim scholars, and was later used by Western philosophers (as in the famous poem of <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a>) who were influenced by the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENasr199659&#8211;60_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENasr199659&#8211;60-161">[148]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_medieval_Europe">On medieval Europe</span></h3>\n<p><span id="Phyllis"></span>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="856" data-file-width="626" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg/170px-Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg/255px-Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg/340px-Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_and_Phyllis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woodcut of <a href="/wiki/The_tale_of_Phyllis_and_Aristotle" title="The tale of Phyllis and Aristotle">Aristotle ridden by Phyllis</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Baldung" title="Hans Baldung">Hans Baldung</a>, 1515<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhyllis_and_Aristotle_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhyllis_and_Aristotle-162">[149]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syllogism#Medieval" title="Syllogism">Syllogism &#167;&#160;Medieval</a></div>\n<p>With the loss of the study of ancient Greek in the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> Latin West, Aristotle was practically unknown there from c. AD 600 to c. 1100 except through the Latin translation of the <i>Organon</i> made by <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>. In the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, interest in Aristotle revived and Latin Christians had translations made, both from Arabic translations, such as those by <a href="/wiki/Gerard_of_Cremona" title="Gerard of Cremona">Gerard of Cremona</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHasse2014_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHasse2014-163">[150]</a></sup> and from the original Greek, such as those by <a href="/wiki/James_of_Venice" title="James of Venice">James of Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Moerbeke" title="William of Moerbeke">William of Moerbeke</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastic</a> Thomas Aquinas wrote his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i>, working from Moerbeke\'s translations and calling Aristotle "The Philosopher",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAquinas2013_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAquinas2013-164">[151]</a></sup> the demand for Aristotle\'s writings grew, and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> manuscripts returned to the West, stimulating a revival of Aristotelianism in Europe that continued into the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuhn2018_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuhn2018-165">[152]</a></sup> These thinkers blended Aristotelian philosophy with Christianity, bringing the thought of Ancient Greece into the Middle Ages. Scholars such as Boethius, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Abelard" title="Peter Abelard">Peter Abelard</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Buridan" title="John Buridan">John Buridan</a> worked on Aristotelian logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELagerlund_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELagerlund-166">[153]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The medieval English poet <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Chaucer</a> describes his student as being happy by having\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"> <div class="poem">\n<p>at his beddes heed<br/>\nTwenty bookes, clad in blak or reed,<br/>\nOf aristotle and his philosophie,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllenFisher201117_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllenFisher201117-167">[154]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/The_tale_of_Phyllis_and_Aristotle" title="The tale of Phyllis and Aristotle">cautionary medieval tale</a> held that Aristotle advised his pupil Alexander to avoid the king\'s seductive mistress, Phyllis, but was himself captivated by her, and allowed her to ride him. Phyllis had secretly told Alexander what to expect, and he witnessed Phyllis proving that a woman\'s charms could overcome even the greatest philosopher\'s male intellect. Artists such as <a href="/wiki/Hans_Baldung" title="Hans Baldung">Hans Baldung</a> produced a series of illustrations of the popular theme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle_Phyllis_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle_Phyllis-168">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhyllis_and_Aristotle_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhyllis_and_Aristotle-162">[149]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Italian poet Dante says of Aristotle in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Divine_Comedy" title="The Divine Comedy">The Divine Comedy</a></i>:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="font-size: 85%;">\n<caption>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a><br/><i>L\'Inferno</i>, Canto IV. 131&#8211;135</th>\n<th>Translation<br/><i>Hell</i>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="poem">\n<p>vidi \'l maestro di color che sanno<br/>\nseder tra filosofica famiglia.<br/>\nTutti lo miran, tutti onor li fanno:<br/>\nquivi vid\'&#239;o Socrate e Platone<br/>\nche \'nnanzi a li altri pi&#249; presso li stanno;\n</p>\n</div></td>\n<td><div class="poem">\n<p>I saw the Master there of those who know,<br/>\nAmid the philosophic family,<br/>\nBy all admired, and by all reverenced;<br/>\nThere Plato too I saw, and Socrates,<br/>\nWho stood beside him closer than the rest.\n</p>\n</div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Early_Modern_scientists">On Early Modern scientists</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="1293" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/220px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/330px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/440px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Exercitatio_Anatomica_de_Motu_Cordis_et_Sanguinis_in_Animalibus" title="Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus">De Motu Cordis</a></i>, 1628, showed that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circulation_of_the_blood" title="Circulation of the blood">blood circulated</a>, contrary to classical era thinking.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern" title="Early Modern">Early Modern</a> period, scientists such as <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a> in England and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> in Italy reacted against the theories of Aristotle and other classical era thinkers like <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, establishing new theories based to some degree on observation and experiment. Harvey demonstrated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circulation_of_the_blood" title="Circulation of the blood">circulation of the blood</a>, establishing that the heart functioned as a pump rather than being the seat of the soul and the controller of the body\'s heat, as Aristotle thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAird2011118&#8211;29_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAird2011118&#8211;29-169">[156]</a></sup> Galileo used more doubtful arguments to displace Aristotle\'s physics, proposing that bodies all fall at the same speed whatever their weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMachamer2017_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMachamer2017-170">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_19th-century_thinkers">On 19th-century thinkers</span></h3>\n<p>The 19th-century German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> has been said to have taken nearly all of his political philosophy from Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurant200686_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurant200686-171">[158]</a></sup> Aristotle rigidly separated action from production, and argued for the deserved subservience of some people ("natural slaves"), and the natural superiority (virtue, <i>arete</i>) of others. It was <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a>, not Nietzsche, who elaborated a new interpretation of Aristotle, intended to warrant his deconstruction of scholastic and philosophical tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESikka1997265_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESikka1997265-172">[159]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The English mathematician <a href="/wiki/George_Boole" title="George Boole">George Boole</a> fully accepted Aristotle\'s logic, but decided "to go under, over, and beyond" it with his system of <a href="/wiki/Boolean_algebra" title="Boolean algebra">algebraic logic</a> in his 1854 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Laws_of_Thought" title="The Laws of Thought">The Laws of Thought</a></i>. This gives logic a mathematical foundation with equations, enables it to solve equations as well as check <a href="/wiki/Validity_(logic)" title="Validity (logic)">validity</a>, and allows it to handle a wider class of problems by expanding propositions of any number of terms, not just two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoole2003_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoole2003-173">[160]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_rejection_and_rehabilitation">Modern rejection and rehabilitation</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg/260px-Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg/390px-Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg/520px-Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Aristotle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"That most enduring of romantic images, Aristotle tutoring the future conqueror Alexander".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77-145">[132]</a></sup> Illustration by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_Laplante&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Charles Laplante (page does not exist)">Charles Laplante</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;[<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Laplante" title="fr:Charles Laplante">fr</a>]</span>, 1866</div></div></div>\n<p>During the 20th century, Aristotle\'s work was widely criticised. The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>\nargued that "almost every serious intellectual advance has had to begin with an attack on some Aristotelian doctrine". Russell called Aristotle\'s ethics "repulsive", and labelled his logic "as definitely antiquated as Ptolemaic astronomy". Russell stated that these errors made it difficult to do historical justice to Aristotle, until one remembered what an advance he made upon all of his predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussell1972_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussell1972-6">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Dutch historian of science <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Jan_Dijksterhuis" title="Eduard Jan Dijksterhuis">Eduard Jan Dijksterhuis</a> wrote that Aristotle and his predecessors showed the difficulty of science by "proceed[ing] so readily to frame a theory of such a general character" on limited evidence from their senses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDijksterhuis196972_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDijksterhuis196972-174">[161]</a></sup> In 1985, the biologist <a href="/wiki/Peter_Medawar" title="Peter Medawar">Peter Medawar</a> could still state in "pure seventeenth century"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015353_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015353-9">[8]</a></sup> tones that Aristotle had assembled "a strange and generally speaking rather tiresome farrago of hearsay, imperfect observation, wishful thinking and credulity amounting to downright gullibility".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015353_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015353-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMedawarMedawar198428_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMedawarMedawar198428-175">[162]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the start of the 21st century, however, Aristotle was taken more seriously: Kukkonen noted that "In the best 20th-century scholarship Aristotle comes alive as a thinker wrestling with the full weight of the Greek philosophical tradition."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77-145">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand">Ayn Rand</a> accredited Aristotle as "the greatest philosopher in history" and cited him as a major influence on her thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMayhew_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMayhew-176">[163]</a></sup> More recently, <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_MacIntyre" title="Alasdair MacIntyre">Alasdair MacIntyre</a> has attempted to reform what he calls the Aristotelian tradition in a way that is anti-elitist and capable of disputing the claims of both liberals and Nietzscheans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnight2007\'\'passim\'\'_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnight2007\'\'passim\'\'-177">[164]</a></sup> Kukkonen observed, too, that "that most enduring of romantic images, Aristotle tutoring the future conqueror Alexander" remained current, as in the 2004 film <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_(2004_film)" title="Alexander (2004 film)">Alexander</a></i>, while the "firm rules" of Aristotle\'s theory of drama have ensured a role for the <i>Poetics</i> in <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">Hollywood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77_145-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKukkonen201070&#8211;77-145">[132]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Biologists continue to be interested in Aristotle\'s thinking. <a href="/wiki/Armand_Marie_Leroi" title="Armand Marie Leroi">Armand Marie Leroi</a> has reconstructed Aristotle\'s biology,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeroi2015_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeroi2015-178">[165]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Tinbergen%27s_four_questions" title="Tinbergen\'s four questions">Niko Tinbergen\'s four questions</a>, based on Aristotle\'s four causes, are used to analyse <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_behaviour" title="Animal behaviour">animal behaviour</a>; they examine <a href="/wiki/Function_(biology)" title="Function (biology)">function</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_(biology)" title="Mechanism (biology)">mechanism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ontogeny" title="Ontogeny">ontogeny</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDougall-Shackleton20112076&#8211;85_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDougall-Shackleton20112076&#8211;85-179">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHladk&#253;Havl&#237;&#269;ek2013_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHladk&#253;Havl&#237;&#269;ek2013-180">[167]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_works">Surviving works</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corpus_Aristotelicum">Corpus Aristotelicum</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Aristotelicum" title="Corpus Aristotelicum">Corpus Aristotelicum</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1025" data-file-width="730" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg/170px-Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg/255px-Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg/340px-Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotelis_De_Moribus_ad_Nicomachum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of a 1566 edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i> in Greek and Latin</div></div></div>\n<p>The works of Aristotle that have survived from antiquity through medieval manuscript transmission are collected in the Corpus Aristotelicum. These texts, as opposed to Aristotle\'s lost works, are technical philosophical treatises from within Aristotle\'s school. Reference to them is made according to the organisation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immanuel_Bekker" title="Immanuel Bekker">Immanuel Bekker</a>\'s Royal Prussian Academy edition (<i>Aristotelis Opera edidit Academia Regia Borussica</i>, Berlin, 1831&#8211;1870), which in turn is based on ancient classifications of these works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotelis_Opera_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotelis_Opera-181">[168]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_and_preservation">Loss and preservation</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Recovery_of_Aristotle" title="Recovery of Aristotle">Recovery of Aristotle</a></div>\n<p>Aristotle wrote his works on papyrus scrolls, the common writing medium of that era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[N]</a></sup> His writings are divisible into two groups: the "<a href="/wiki/Exoteric" title="Exoteric">exoteric</a>", intended for the public, and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esoteric" title="Esoteric">esoteric</a>", for use within the <a href="/wiki/Lyceum_(Classical)" title="Lyceum (Classical)">Lyceum</a> school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnes199512_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnes199512-184">[170]</a></sup><span class="error mw-ext-cite-error" dir="ltr" lang="en">Cite error: There are <code>&lt;ref&gt;</code> tags on this page without content in them (see the <a href="/wiki/Help:Cite_errors/Cite_error_ref_no_input" title="Help:Cite errors/Cite error ref no input">help page</a>).\n</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHouse195635_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHouse195635-185">[171]</a></sup> Aristotle\'s "lost" works stray considerably in characterisation from the surviving Aristotelian corpus. Whereas the lost works appear to have been originally written with a view to subsequent publication, the surviving works mostly resemble lecture notes not intended for publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrwinFine1996xi&#8211;xii_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrwinFine1996xi&#8211;xii-186">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnes199512_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnes199512-184">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>\'s description of Aristotle\'s literary style as "a river of gold" must have applied to the published works, not the surviving notes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[O]</a></sup> A major question in the history of Aristotle\'s works is how the exoteric writings were all lost, and how the ones we now possess came to us.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnesGriffin19991&#8211;69_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnesGriffin19991&#8211;69-189">[174]</a></sup> The consensus is that Andronicus of Rhodes collected the esoteric works of Aristotle\'s school which existed in the form of smaller, separate works, distinguished them from those of Theophrastus and other Peripatetics, edited them, and finally compiled them into the more cohesive, larger works as they are known today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnagnostopoulos201316_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnagnostopoulos201316-190">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnes199510&#8211;15_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnes199510&#8211;15-191">[176]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Depictions">Depictions</span></h3>\n<p>Aristotle has been depicted by major artists including <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucas_Cranach_the_Elder_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucas_Cranach_the_Elder-192">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Justus_van_Gent" title="Justus van Gent">Justus van Gent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Veronese" title="Paolo Veronese">Paolo Veronese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jusepe_de_Ribera" title="Jusepe de Ribera">Jusepe de Ribera</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeeRobinson2005_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeeRobinson2005-193">[178]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle_with_Bust2002_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle_with_Bust2002-194">[179]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Hayez" title="Francesco Hayez">Francesco Hayez</a> over the centuries. Among the best-known is Raphael\'s <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i>, in the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Palace" title="Apostolic Palace">Vatican\'s Apostolic Palace</a>, where the figures of Plato and Aristotle are central to the image, at the architectural <a href="/wiki/Vanishing_point" title="Vanishing point">vanishing point</a>, reflecting their importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhelan2002_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhelan2002-195">[180]</a></sup> Rembrandt\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aristotle_with_a_Bust_of_Homer" title="Aristotle with a Bust of Homer">Aristotle with a Bust of Homer</a></i>, too, is a celebrated work, showing the knowing philosopher and the blind Homer from an earlier age: as the art critic <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Jones_(journalist)" title="Jonathan Jones (journalist)">Jonathan Jones</a> writes, "this painting will remain one of the greatest and most mysterious in the world, ensnaring us in its musty, glowing, pitch-black, terrible knowledge of time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeld1969_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeld1969-196">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2002_197-0"><a
