href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2002-197">[182]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 124.66666666667px"><div style="width: Oil on canvas, 1653\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 144px"><div style="width: 144px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 142px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johann_Jakob_Dorner_d_%C3%84_(attr)_Aristoteles.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4701" data-file-width="3921" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Johann_Jakob_Dorner_d_%C3%84_%28attr%29_Aristoteles.jpg/213px-Johann_Jakob_Dorner_d_%C3%84_%28attr%29_Aristoteles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Johann_Jakob_Dorner_d_%C3%84_%28attr%29_Aristoteles.jpg/319px-Johann_Jakob_Dorner_d_%C3%84_%28attr%29_Aristoteles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Johann_Jakob_Dorner_d_%C3%84_%28attr%29_Aristoteles.jpg/425px-Johann_Jakob_Dorner_d_%C3%84_%28attr%29_Aristoteles.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i>Aristotle</i> by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Jakob_Dorner_the_Elder" title="Johann Jakob Dorner the Elder">Johann Jakob Dorner the Elder</a>. Oil on canvas, by 1813\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 134px"><div style="width: 134px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 132px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2604" data-file-width="2024" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg/198px-Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg/298px-Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg/396px-Francesco_Hayez_001.jpg 2x" width="132"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i>Aristotle</i> by <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Hayez" title="Francesco Hayez">Francesco Hayez</a>. Oil on canvas, 1811\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eponyms">Eponyms</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Mountains" title="Aristotle Mountains">Aristotle Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> are named after Aristotle. He was the first person known to conjecture, in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Meteorology_(Aristotle)" title="Meteorology (Aristotle)">Meteorology</a></i>, the existence of a landmass in the southern high-latitude region and called it <i>Antarctica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristotle_Mountains_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristotle_Mountains-199">[183]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aristoteles_(crater)" title="Aristoteles (crater)">Aristoteles</a> is a crater on the Moon bearing the classical form of Aristotle\'s name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAristoteles_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAristoteles-200">[184]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portal-puzzle.svg"><img alt="Portal-puzzle.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/16px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/24px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/32px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Aristotle" title="Portal:Aristotle">Aristotle portal</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_Society" title="Aristotelian Society">Aristotelian Society</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Conimbricenses" title="Conimbricenses">Conimbricenses</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: upper-alpha;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That these dates (the first half of the Olympiad year 384/383 BC, and in 322 shortly before the death of Demosthenes) are correct was shown by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/August_Boeckh" title="August Boeckh">August Boeckh</a> (<i>Kleine Schriften</i> VI 195); for further discussion, see <a href="/wiki/Felix_Jacoby" title="Felix Jacoby">Felix Jacoby</a> on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FGrHist" title="FGrHist">FGrHist</a></i> 244 F 38. Ingemar D&#252;ring, <i>Aristotle in the Ancient Biographical Tradition</i>, G&#246;teborg, 1957,p.&#160; 253</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See(<a href="#CITEREFShields2012">Shields 2012</a>, pp.&#160;3&#8211;16);(<a href="#CITEREFD&#252;ring1957">D&#252;ring 1957</a>) covers ancient biographies of Aristotle.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This <a href="/wiki/Syllogism#Types" title="Syllogism">type of syllogism</a>, with all three terms in \'a\', is known by the traditional (medieval) <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">mnemonic</a> B<b>a</b>rb<b>a</b>r<b>a</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016-30">[28]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">M is the Middle (here, Men), S is the Subject (Greeks), P is the Predicate (mortal).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELagerlund2016-30">[28]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first equation can be read as \'It is not true that there exists an x such that x is a man and that x is not mortal.\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPredicate_Logic_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPredicate_Logic-33">[29]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rhett Allain notes that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_First_Law" title="Newton\'s First Law">Newton\'s First Law</a> is "essentially a direct reply to Aristotle, that the natural state is <i>not to change</i> motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllain2016_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllain2016-49">[44]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Leonard Susskind comments that Aristotle had clearly never gone <a href="/wiki/Ice_skating" title="Ice skating">ice skating</a> or he would have seen that it takes force to stop an object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESusskind2011_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESusskind2011-52">[46]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For heavenly bodies like the Sun, Moon, and stars, the observed motions are "to a very good approximation" circular around the Earth\'s centre, (for example, the apparent rotation of the sky because of the rotation of the Earth, and the rotation of the moon around the Earth) as Aristotle stated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Drabkin quotes numerous passages from <i>Physics</i> and <i>On the Heavens</i> (<i>De Caelo</i>) which state Aristotle\'s laws of motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Drabkin agrees that density is treated quantitatively in this passage, but without a sharp definition of density as weight per unit volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrabkin193860&#8211;84-51">[45]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Philoponus and Galileo correctly objected that for the transient phase (still increasing in speed) with heavy objects falling a short distance, the law does not apply: Galileo used balls on a short incline to show this. Rovelli notes that "Two heavy balls with the same shape and different weight do fall at different speeds from an aeroplane, confirming Aristotle\'s theory, not Galileo\'s."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40_54-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERovelli201523&#8211;40-54">[47]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a different reading of social and economic processes in the <i>Nicomachean Ethics</i> and <i>Politics</i> see Polanyi, Karl (1957) "Aristotle Discovers the Economy" in <i>Primitive, Archaic and Modern Economies: Essays of Karl Polanyi</i> ed. G. Dalton, Boston 1971, 78&#8211;115</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Where, as among the Lacedaemonians, the state of women is bad, almost half of human life is spoilt."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhetoricBook_I,_Chapter_5_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhetoricBook_I,_Chapter_5-137">[125]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-183"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-183">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"When the Roman dictator Sulla invaded Athens in 86 BC, he brought back to Rome a fantastic prize &#8211; Aristotle\'s library. Books then were papyrus rolls, from 10 to 20 feet long, and since Aristotle\'s death in 322 BC, worms and damp had done their worst. The rolls needed repairing, and the texts clarifying and copying on to new papyrus (imported from Egypt &#8211; Moses\' bulrushes). The man in Rome who put Aristotle\'s library in order was a Greek scholar, Tyrannio."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhen_libraries_were2001_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhen_libraries_were2001-182">[169]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-188"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-188">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<i>veniet flumen orationis aureum fundens Aristoteles</i>", (Google translation: "Aristotle will come pouring forth a golden stream of eloquence").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECicero1874_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECicero1874-187">[173]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-198"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-198">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare the medieval tale of Phyllis and Alexander above.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline"
